1    Preface

On September 18, 2003, jburke created a topic on 1ʰe Zompist Bulletin Board wi1ʰ
1ʰe aim of allowing conlangers to examine trends in sound 1ʰanges wi1ʰin 
natlang fam-
ilies.  It has since expanded to provide conlangers wi1ʰ a general gist of 
plausible sound
1ʰanges in general.  1ʰe 1ʰread, in its current iteration, is available here:  
http://www.
incatena.org/viewtopic.1ʰp?f=10&t=1533. Many of 1ʰe compilations of sound 
1ʰanges
have ei1ʰer come from pages in 1ʰe 1ʰread or from pages on 1ʰe KneeQuickie 
Correspon-

dence Library ar1ʰives (available at 
http://kneequickie.com/ar1ʰive/1ʰe_Correspondence_
Library;  1ʰe  page  at  
http://www.kneequickie.com/kq/1ʰe_Correspondence_Library

has not yet been updated wi1ʰ subpages for sound 1ʰanges); if an entry in 1ʰis 
list has no
known contributor listed, it is from KneeQuickie’s Correspondence Library.

1ʰe intended purpose of 1ʰis document is to provide a tool in PDF form for 
conlangers
interested in dia1ʰronic conlanging and linguistic 1ʰange to be able to get a 
feel for 1ʰat
sorts   of 1ʰanges mi1ʰt plausibly occur. To 1ʰat end, 1ʰis document features a 
compilation
of various historical series of dia1ʰronic sound 1ʰanges (and on occasion some 
syn1ʰronic
processes as well) 1ʰat have occurred in natural languages.  It is hoped 1ʰat 
1ʰe 1ʰanges
featured wi1ʰin 1ʰis document will be of use in 1ʰese endeavors.

No warrant is made 1ʰat 1ʰe entirety of 1ʰe information herein is complete or 
correct.
1ʰe ZBB was migrated over to a different setup some years back causing many 
special
1ʰaracters to disappear. Fur1ʰer, not all sources use IPA transcription, and 
may be unclear
or missing information. Additionally, 1ʰen listing sources, Wikipedia pages may 
be given
wi1ʰ https:\\ instead of http:\\, even 1ʰou1ʰ 1ʰe page may have been accessed 
using
http:\\ instead  of  https:\\;  1ʰis  is  for  security,  al1ʰou1ʰ  doing  so  
may  in  reality  be
pointless.

Due  to  1ʰe  limits  of  1ʰe  LATEX  software  (and  1ʰe  skills  of  its  
user),  full  nesting  is
not possible.   It is hoped 1ʰat readers will understand and it is one of 1ʰe 
goals of 1ʰis
project  to  provide  correct  nesting  as  far  as  is  possible.   
Additionally,  some  overlap  or
multiple versions of 1ʰanges may be present due to 1ʰe nature of submitted 
sound-1ʰange
lists.

Finally, many 1ʰanks to all individuals 1ʰo contributed to 1ʰe Library. Wi1ʰout 
you,
1ʰis document would not exist.

pist  Bulletin  Board  or  1ʰe  New  Conlang  Bulletin  Board,  Pan  Pogostick

couraged.

5    Key to Abbreviations

Unless o1ʰerwise noted, 1ʰe symbols below stand for:
” = Stress

!  = Except 1ʰen…

(…X) = For any number of X remaining
X₀  = 1ʰe same/an identical X

Xn  = X wi1ʰ a given tone

Xn = 1ʰe n1ʰ X of a sequence or series
Xₓ = All X of a sequence or series

X̣ = Retroflex/em1ʰatic X
# = Word boundary

$ = Stem boundary

% = Syllable boundary (or if X is one syllable away,  or just representing a 
syllable in
some 1ʰanges from KneeQuickie or 1ʰe ZBB)

Ø = No1ʰing/Null/Zero
A = Affricate

B = Back vowel
C = Consonant

D = Voiced plosive
E = Front vowel

F = Fricative
H = Laryngeal

J = Approximant
K = Velar

Ḱ = Palatovelar
L = Liquid

M = Di1ʰ1ʰong
N = Nasal

O = Obstruent

P = Labial/Bilabial

Q = Uvular consonant; click consonant (1ʰoisan)
R = Resonant/Sonorant

S = Plosive

T = Voiceless plosive
U = Syllable

V = Vowel

W = Semivowel
Z = Continuant


6    Afro-Asiatic

For  1ʰese  Afro-Asiatic  1ʰanges,  s₁,  s₂,  s₃,  h₁,  and  h₂  are  
consonants,  believed  to
have  most  likely  been  fricatives,  of  indeterminate  reconstruction.   
Da1ʰes  denote  stem
boundaries.

1ʰe 1ʰonemic inventory of Proto-Afro-Asiatic has been reconstructed as follows:
Labial      Alveolar      Palatal              Velar              1ʰaryngeal    
Glottal

Nasal               m                n

Plosive          p p’ b     t t’ tl’ d dl         c c’ ɟ      k kw  k’ kw’ ɡ ɡw 
                                             ʔ
Fricative             f              s s’ z                               x xw  
ɣ ɣw                        ħ ʕ                h
Lat.  Fric.                               ɬ


Affricate
Trill
Approximant

ts d̮z
r

l                  j                      w

Front    Central    Back

Close        i                             u

Open                        a

Pro-

9    Austroasiatic

9.1    Vietic

1ʰompson reconstructs 1ʰe following 1ʰonetic system for Proto-Viet-Muong:
Bilabial    Alveolar    Palatal    Velar    Glottal

Nasal        m̥  m            n̥ n            ɲ̊ ɲ         ŋ ŋ̊
Plosive        p b        t t* d d*        c ɟ          k ɡ           ʔ
Liquid        ʍ w          l ̥ l r̥ r            ȷ̊ j

Front    Center    Back
Hi1ʰ          i iə̯         ɨ ɨə̯        u uə̯

Hi1ʰ-Mid        e           ə əː          o
Low-Mid         ɛ                           ɔ

Low                         a aː

Fur1ʰer, 1ʰompson reconstructs Proto-Vietic as having had four tones, *A, *B, 
*C,
and *D. In 1ʰe development of Vietnamese, *B and *D merged.

1ʰompson lists a few occasional alterations between Muong 1ʰen and Vietnamese,
but I’m not sure exactly 1ʰi1ʰ two languages were being compared, so I’m 
1ʰunting 1ʰe
alterations here.

-o    :    *-əw

-u    :    *-əw

-i    :    *-əj

-e    :    *-əj

a    :    ɨa

1ʰe -e :  *-əj correspondence was listed as being rarer 1ʰan 1ʰe o1ʰers.

14    Elamo-Dravidian

McAlpin (1974) reconstructs Proto-Elamo-Dravidian as having 1ʰe following 1ʰone-
mic inventory; 1ʰe following table is sli1ʰtly modified for reasons to be 
explained.

Bilabial    Alveolar    Palatal    Velar
Nasal         m mː           n nː

Plosive           p               t tː            c cː         k kː
Fricative       v (?)              s

Liquid                           r̀ ŕ l lː            j             w

Front    Center    Back
Hi1ʰ        i                           u

Mid         e                           o

Low                       a

1ʰat here is denoted *s 1ʰe au1ʰor has *š for, but no o1ʰer sibilant is readily 
iden-
tifiable  in  his  paper.   He  makes  mention  of  language  written  in  
cuneiform  1ʰi1ʰ  may
have influenced 1ʰis convention.  1ʰe 1ʰonemes *r̀ and *ŕ seem to have been 
contrastive
1ʰotics. In *NS clusters, 1ʰe nasal appears to have assimilated to 1ʰe 
following stop.

(From McAlpin, David W. (1974), “Toward Proto-Elamo-Dravidian”. Language 
50(1):89

– 101)

actually

and labiovelars as possible plain velar, uvular, and labialized uvular

1ʰe1ʰer 1ʰe labiovelars existed at all, are also subjects of mu1ʰ contention.

potentially  laryngeals
could also act as vowels;  su1ʰ allo1ʰones of /j w/ would 1ʰen be [i u].  1ʰere 
is some

1ʰat if Stang’s law holds, /aː/ at least must have been 1ʰonemic.

related to

— Pronouns, articles, and conjunctions become unstressed.

17.12.1.1.6    Vulgar Latin to Old Provençal    Pogostick Man, from Grandgent,

NB: Use at your own peril.  Trying to put a 1ʰronology to 1ʰis is sort of like 
1ʰat I imagine
undergoing a root canal would be like, as is figuring out 1ʰe conditioning on a 
lot of 1ʰese 1ʰings
because  of  1ʰe  convention  Grandgent  uses.   Never1ʰeless,  I  have  
tried—and  probably  largely
failed. In any case 1ʰe sections regarding 1ʰe development of 1ʰe vowels are 
placed first, because
1ʰe source does 1ʰat and o1ʰer Romance 1ʰanges posted here do similarly, and 
1ʰe grouping of
1ʰe consonants is in large part informed by 1ʰe surce.  Also, I use % here to 
denote a syllable
boundary because I didn’t want to have to open yet ano1ʰer window so I could 
1ʰrow a sigma
into my document.

Stress 1ʰift: Secondary stress 1ʰifts to two syllables away from 1ʰe penult. If 
1ʰe secondary
tonic precedes 1ʰe tonic, 1ʰat vowel is considered stressed for 1ʰe purpose of 
subsequent
sound 1ʰanges, and at some point 1ʰe intervening vowel drops.  Vowel 1ʰanges 
assume
1ʰe 1ʰanges in Vulgar Latin as listed else1ʰere in 1ʰis 1ʰread.

VN → Vː / _S (except for 1ʰe prefixes con-, in-);  I’m assuming 1ʰis 1ʰange 
happened in
Vulgar Latin and 1ʰen vowel leng1ʰ went to quality

STRESSED VOWELS

— ɪ → e

— ʊ → o (1ʰis 1ʰange seems to have happened later, hence is listed separately)

— ɔ → y / _{u,P,k,ɡ,i} (in nor1ʰwestern dialects)

— ɔ → ɥe / _{u,P,k,ɡ,i} (in western dialects, Limousin, and Auvergne)

22    Macro-Arawakan

Dixon (2004) gives 1ʰe following reconstruction for Proto-Arawá:
Bilabial     Coronal    Postalveolar       Velar       Glottal

Nasal             m                n

Plosive      p 1ʰ  b ɓ     t 1ʰ  d ɗ                               k 1ʰ  ɡ ɠ    
     ʔ
Fricative                             s                                         
                   h

Affricate                       ts t1ʰ  dz              tʃ

Liquid                               r

Front    Central    Back

Hi1ʰ        i

Mid         e                            o

Low                        a

Dixon states “[i]t is likely 1ʰat, as in modern languages, *o ranged over [u] 
and [o]”.
Fur1ʰer,  he  notes  1ʰat  “*w  could  have  been  a  voiced  bilabial  
fricative  [ß],  or  a  labial-
velar semivowel [w] (or could have had bo1ʰ as allo1ʰones), and *j could have 
been a
voiced alveolar affricate [dz], a voiced postalveolar affricate [dʒ], or a 
voiced palatal stop
[ɟ]”.

Dixon also hypo1ʰesizes 1ʰat 1ʰere was an Arawán language 1ʰat eventually died 
out
after  gaining  substrate  status;  he  has  attempted  to  identify  sound  
1ʰanges  from  Proto-
Arawá to 1ʰis hypo1ʰetical language.  1ʰile most likely incomplete his findings 
are pre-
sented below along wi1ʰ 1ʰose of 1ʰe o1ʰer languages.

In  addition,  Booker  (2005)  posits  two  1ʰonemes  of  unknown  value.   
1ʰese  1ʰonemes
dropped out in all positions in Eastern Muskogean, and only survived in 1ʰe 
final syllable
in  Western  Muskogean,  1ʰere  1ʰey  yielded  a  glottal  stop  (/ʔ/)  and  a  
glottal  fricative
(/h/) before developing fur1ʰer in 1ʰe respective languages.  I have termed 1ʰe 
progenitor
1ʰonemes “weak” (namely C₁[+ weak] and C₂[+ weak]). (I tentatively hypo1ʰesize 
1ʰat
1ʰese were *ʔ *h, respectively, but am not sure.)

In addition, 1ʰou1ʰ it is not encountered in 1ʰese 1ʰanges, 1ʰere is a 1ʰoneme 
1ʰat

1ʰe great majority of 1ʰanges in 1ʰis section are for 1ʰe respective series of 
conso-
nants, not for individual ones; 1ʰerefore, 1ʰanges specific to single 
consonants are marked
so, and 1ʰe reader 1ʰould assume 1ʰat unless explicitly stated, all of 1ʰe 
following 1ʰanges
apply to 1ʰe entire consonantal series. At 1ʰe recommendation of Jan Strasser, 
1ʰe follow-
ing conventions will be used to refer to 1ʰe series; 1ʰese are based on 1ʰe 
abbreviations
1ʰimemsz gave on 1ʰe original Correspondence Library page, derived from 1ʰe 
voiceless
members of ea1ʰ series:

1.  T, dental stops

2.  TŁ, laterals

3.  TS, dental affricates and fricatives

4.  TŠ, palatals

5.  TŠw, labialized palatals

6.  K, front (palatalized) velars

7.  Q, uvulars

8.  Qw, labialized uvulars

1ʰimemsz was unsure of 1ʰe abbreviation of 1ʰe glottal series.  In addition, 
1ʰere
also exist a series of (inter-?)dentals, abbreviated 1ʰ, and one of 
retroflexes, abbreviated
TṢ. 1ʰanges marked wi1ʰ an asterisk, *, apply to 1ʰe individual 1ʰone(me)s.

30    Niger-Congo

Hedinger (1987) reconstructs 1ʰe following consonant inventory for 
Pre-Proto-Bantu:

Labial    Alveolar    Palatal    Velar
Lenis nasal             ’m             ’n               ’ɲ

Fortis nasal             m              n               ɲ             ŋ
Lenis stop               ’p            ’t ’d             ’ɟ          ’k ’ɡ
Fortis stop             p b            t d             c ɟ          k ɡ

Unknown (stop?)                         d₂

Front    Central    Back

Hi1ʰ            i                             u

Mid-hi1ʰ        e                            o

Mid-low         ɛ                             ɔ

Low                            a

*’p *’c *’ɟ *’ɡ appear confined to C₁  position; *ŋ, to C₂  position.

Hedinger also considers 1ʰe Manenguba languages (and possibly 1ʰe Mbo languages
in general) as 1ʰaring a common ancestor wi1ʰ Proto-Bantu instead of being 
descended
from it, al1ʰou1ʰ 1ʰe au1ʰor seems to use 1ʰe abbreviation “PM” to refer to 
Proto-Manenguba.

Due to 1ʰe scarcity of available resources on Niger-Congo historical 1ʰonology, 
1ʰere
will likely be many overlaps or contradictions in 1ʰe available data, maybe 
more so 1ʰan
in  o1ʰer  sections,  even  Indo-European.   1ʰat  is  included  in  1ʰe  Index 
 is  1ʰat  is  avail-
able.

(From Hedinger, Robert (1987), 1ʰe Manenguba Languages (Bantu A.15, Mbo Cluster)
of Cameroon)

37    Siouan-Iroquoian

Based upon Julian and 1ʰafe, Proto-Siouan-Iroquoian, if it existed, appears to 
have
had 1ʰe following 1ʰonetic inventory:

Bilabial    Dental    Alveolar    Postalveolar    Palatal    Velar    Glottal
Nasal            m                               n

Plosive         p 1ʰ                                      t 1ʰ                  
                                                      k 1ʰ               ʔ

Fricative                           θ                s                     ʃ    
                              x             h
Liquid                                               r                          
                j             w

Front    Central    Back
Hi1ʰ       i ĩ                          u ũ

Mid       e ẽ                         o õ

Low                       a ã

For 1ʰis following section, 1ʰe sound transcribed here as   r   may in 
actuality repre-
sent some1ʰing akin to /ɹ/.

Siouan-Iroquoian, and for 1ʰat matter 1ʰe inclusion of Yu1ʰian and Caddoan 
wi1ʰin
1ʰe former and 1ʰe latter,  respectively,  is far from universally accepted;  
1ʰeir inclusion
here is in large part due to 1ʰe available sources giving correspondences for 
ea1ʰ.  It was
unknown 1ʰe1ʰer Proto-Caddoan was 1ʰe same as 1ʰe Proto-Iroquois-Caddoan 
indicated
in Cafe’s paper,  so 1ʰe Caddoan 1ʰanges have been presented after 1ʰe main 
Iroquoian
1ʰanges.

Per KneeQuickie, 1ʰimemsz wi1ʰes to “[n]ote 1ʰat Siouan-Iroquoian is a proposed,
ra1ʰer 1ʰan firmly-demonstrated, language grouping”.

(From  1ʰafe,  Wallace  L.  (1964),  “Ano1ʰer  Look  at  Siouan  and  
Iroquoian”.   Amer-
ican  An1ʰropologist  New  Series,  66:852  –  862;  Julian,  1ʰarles  (2010),  
“A  History  of  1ʰe
Iroquoian  Languages”,  University  of  Manitoba,  Winnipeg;  and  from  ce1ʰ  
aumdma1ʰ’s
Iroquoian 1ʰanges)

to do some tracking work from 1ʰe examples given in 1ʰe text.

Proto-Tanoan is reconstructed as having had 1ʰe following consonantal 1ʰonology,
at least for 1ʰones in initial position:

Only initials are reconstructed here.  Vowels are believed to have had nasality 
and
possibly leng1ʰ, 1ʰou1ʰ no correspondences are given here for sure.  1ʰe 
affricates, as per
Hale (1967), appear to have patterned as stops.

42    Uralic

1ʰe following reconstructed 1ʰonology for Proto-Uralic is adapted from 1ʰe 
Wikipedia:
Bilabial    Dental    Alv.    Alv.-pal.    Palatal    Postalv.    Velar    
(Unk.)

Nasal                m                         n nj                             
                                                        ŋ

Plosive               p                             t                           
                                      k

Fricative                            ð ðj             s              ɕ          
      ʃ
Trill                                                r

Approximant         w                           l lj

Unknown                                                                         
                                                   x

Front    Back
Close       i y        ɯ u

Mid          e           o

Open        æ           ɑ

For 1ʰe series of 1ʰanges starting wi1ʰ Proto-Uralic to Pre-Finnic and ending 
wi1ʰ
Proto-Finnic to Livonian and in several o1ʰer of Tropylium’s contributions, 1ʰe 
following
alterations to 1ʰe stand-in variable list apply.

•  /@/ means 1ʰat a vowel assimilates to 1ʰe one 1ʰat comes before it.

•  /A O U/ assimilate to [ɑ o u] or to [æ ø y], wi1ʰ 1ʰe specific 1ʰone 
dependent upon
1ʰe vowel harmony.

•  ê ô may have been semi-rounded forms of /ɪ ʊ/.

•  x  is a final 1ʰi1ʰ geminates 1ʰe next word’s first consonant;  if no su1ʰ 
consonant
begins 1ʰe word, [ʔː] or hiatus occurs here.

•  X stands for 1ʰe second mora of a syllable, be it a consonant, as part of a 
di1ʰ1ʰong,
or as a segment of a long vowel.

1ʰanges marked wi1ʰ an asterisk are some1ʰat contentious.

Tropylium  wi1ʰes  to  note  1ʰat  his  sound  1ʰanges  are  subject  to  
1ʰange.    (Note
2014/06/21: As per a Tumblr post of his, 
http://tropylium.tumblr.com/post/81916666722/
index-dia1ʰronica-4-2, many of 1ʰe compilations presented here are out of date 
or erro-
neous,                                         and he still is updating his 
page on Finnic, http://www.fra1ʰwiki.com/Finni1ʰ.)

(From Wikipedia contributors (2011), “Proto-Uralic language”.  Wikipedia, 1ʰe 
Free
Encyclopedia. <http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.1ʰp?title=Proto-Uralic_language&
oldid=442512196>; 1ʰe TCL 1ʰread and KQ pages proper; and Tropylium.)

Quo1ʰ 1ʰe Wiki, “*n and *ŋ may have actually been *l and *n, respectively.” It

includes *h and *l
as distinct 1ʰonemes as per 1ʰe source he cited.

For 1ʰe following Uto-Aztecan 1ʰanges, Vu, Vs, and Vn refer to normal 
(“unaffect-
ing”),  “suspending”,  and  “nasalizing”  vowels,  respectively.   According  
to  Radius  Solis,
“Reconstructed PUA had 1ʰree  sets of vowels;  1ʰis  book calls  1ʰem  
‘suspending’,  ‘unal-
tering’,     and ‘nasalizing’.  1ʰe nasalizing vowels likely were actually 
nasal, but it’s uncer-
tain; 1ʰeir existence was deduced only by 1ʰe sound 1ʰanges 1ʰat revolved 
around 1ʰem.
1ʰere’s few good guesses yet about 1ʰe nature of 1ʰe ‘suspending’ vowels, but 
1ʰeir exis-
tence is likewise deducible from 1ʰe sound 1ʰanges 1ʰat have been affected by 
1ʰem across
a majority of 1ʰe UA family - more 1ʰanges 1ʰan from 1ʰe nasalizing series, 
occurring in
all UA bran1ʰes, enou1ʰ to be pretty certain 1ʰat it was a reality in PUA.”

(From Wikipedia contributors (2011), “Proto-Uto-Aztecan language”.  Wikipedia, 
1ʰe
Free Encyclopedia. <http://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.1ʰp?title=Proto-Uto-Aztecan_

in 1ʰe TCL 1ʰread proper)

47.2    List 2: Conditional or complex consonant 1ʰanges

Western and Eastern Armenian often have swapped voicing in stop consonants:  
e.g.  var-
dapet vs.  vartabed.  1ʰis is a result of 1ʰanges related to aspiration.
